        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'f8d25851-dc4f-432b-8c04-95cd7ece0b77'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:nanaw@uow.edu.au"><span>nanaw@uow.edu.au</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:baizhongchao@tyut.edu.cn"><span>baizhongchao@tyut.edu.cn</span></a><a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:yangjian@sdu.edu.cn"><span>yangjian@sdu.edu.cn</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Wang/Nana">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Bai/Zhongchao" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a6" data-db-target-for="a6" aria-controls="a6" aria-haspopup="true" id="a6_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Zhongchao Bai<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a6" aria-labelledby="a6_Ctrl" role="region" id="a6">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d81318296" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>TiO<sub>2</sub>-based materials are considered to be the promising anodes of sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) because of their high safety and good stability. However, their low specific capacity and high safety operating voltage plateau impose a severe challenge for high energy density batteries. Herein, interconnected micro-sheets consisting of carbon nanotubes and sulfur doped TiO<sub>2</sub> (CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub>) are synthesized via an ultrasonic process and subsequent calcination, enabling the fabrication of high-performance material. The utilization of SWCNT overcomes the structure instabilities during electrode preparation of thick electrodes. The incorporation of SWCNT and sulfur dopants in the CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> composite not only enhances conductivity but also improves ion transport dynamics, resulting in rapid charge delivery and high specific capacity at the thick electrode level. Consequently, CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> demonstrates excellent rate performance (from 0.3 to 15&nbsp;C, with 72.4% capacity retention) and long cycling stability (10000 cycles at a load of 1.96&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>). More importantly, the high S-TiO<sub>2</sub> content (90%) in the thick electrode (21.2&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>) achieves a high areal capacity retention of 3.4&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> after 100 cycles, which surpasses the actual application requirements.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202301996-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202301996-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) have gained significant attention as a highly promising source of large-scale energy storage due to its cost-effectiveness, eco-friendliness, and the abundance of raw materials in the earth's crust.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0001_R_d81318288e487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nature Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Solid State Ion Energy Mater">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the graphite anode in commercially available lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) has demonstrated inadequate sodium storage capacity due to the larger size of sodium ions (1.02&nbsp;Å) compared to lithium ions (0.76&nbsp;Å).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0002_R_d81318288e492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Rev.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the development of safe, stable, and cost-effective anode materials is imperative for the commercialization of NIBs. In this regard, titanium-based materials are a desirable option for NIBs due to a number of advantageous factors, including a desirable operating potential (0.3–1&nbsp;V) that can mitigate safety concerns associated with sodium dendrites, economic benefits, environmental sustainability, non-toxicity, and structural stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0003_R_d81318288e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Commun. Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless,the utilization of titanium-based materials in NIBsis challenged by low conductivity resulting from their substantial bandgap, such as 3.7&nbsp;eV for sodium titanate and 3.2&nbsp;eV for the anatase structure, and a reduced capacity brought by slow sodium mobility that results in the preferential occurrence of the sodium ion insertion reaction at the surface layer of these materials. This typically results in a trade-off between high safety and high storage capacity. Therefore, a way to achieve both high safety and high storage capacity must be found.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0004_R_d81318288e503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Carbon Energy">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The present studies mainly focus on improving the specific capacity of electrode materials by hetero-atom doping, size control, and hierarchical structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0005_R_d81318288e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - ACS Nano J Energy Chem">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These strategies have made remarkable progress in electrochemical performance. Some reports even approached the theoretical limits of TiO<sub>2</sub> (335&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). Nevertheless, these results are achieved using nano-sized materials and under low mass loading, typically ≈1.0&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, which does not fit the practical applications. In practice, the electrode needs to deliver an areal capacity of at least 3&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0006_R_d81318288e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Science J. Power Sources">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the mass loading of TiO<sub>2</sub> in the electrode has to be &gt;10&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. Such a thick electrode would face great challenges upon cycles. The first one is to achieve the uniform dispersion of TiO<sub>2</sub>, polymer binder, and conductive carbon in the thick electrode by a simple and scalable protocol.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0007_R_d81318288e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Energy Adv. Energy Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Second, it is crucial to maintain efficient electron/ion transportation in the thick electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0008_R_d81318288e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Third, the thick electrode preserves the structure stability and mitigates the performance degradation upon cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0009_R_d81318288e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Eng. J.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To date, several methods have been developed to address these issues, such as current collector optimizing,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0010_R_d81318288e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J Energy Chem E.Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> free-standing carriers,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0006_R_d81318288e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Science J. Power Sources">6</a>, <a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0011_R_d81318288e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Mater. Lett.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> oriented freezing,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0012_R_d81318288e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Commun.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 3D printing,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0013_R_d81318288e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Stor. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc. However, these methods always involve complicated and cumbersome preparation. Therefore, a novel approach is highly desirable for thick and high-performance electrodes.</p>
         
         <p>Here, we designed and fabricated a highly conductive CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> sheet constructed by S-TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles and single-wall carbon nanotubes (SWCNT) via a facile and scalable heating process. When used as anodes for SIBs, such unique 2D electrode structures have several benefits. First, the presence of SWCNT can enhance the mechanical stability and conductivity of the electrode framework, allowing fewer additives (binders) to maximize the content of active substances in the electrode. Second, the primary S-TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles and large aspect ratio of SWCNT enable a homogeneous electron conductive matrix, and the voids inside CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> can provide rapid ion transportation pathways during the sodiation/desodiation process, especially in thick electrodes. Third, the traditional polymer binder and conductive agent are replaced, which effectively avoids the issues of server cracking and uneven dispersion of electroactive materials. As a result, CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> exhibits excellent electrochemical performance, including superior rate capability (capacity retention of 72.4% at 15&nbsp;C), excellent cycling stability (over 10&nbsp;000 cycles at 15&nbsp;C), and a high areal capacity of 3.4&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> over 100&nbsp;cycles.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202301996-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202301996-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0001">1a</a>, compared to the conventional protocols, the thick electrode should have robust structure stability, high electronic/ionic conductivity, and fast mass transportation. Therefore, we design a micro-sheet network composite of carbon nanotubes with sulfur doped TiO<sub>2</sub> (CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub>), which can fulfill&nbsp;the requirements of thick electrode. These micro-sheets were obtained by a facile process, using polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP), TiOSO<sub>4</sub>, and SWCNTs as the reactants. PVP, which serves as a dispersant, was used to ensure good dispersion of SWCNTs in water (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). TiOSO<sub>4</sub> served as the precursor of S-TiO<sub>2</sub> and the S sources for heterogeneous element doping. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0001">1b,c</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), the TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles and SWCNT were successfully assembled into a sheet-like structure during the preparation process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0014_R_d81318288e627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Cross-sectional SEM images demonstrate that carbon nanotubes uniformly distribute between the TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles. TEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0001">1d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) indicate that TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles (≈40&nbsp;nm) are tightly linked by carbon nanotubes, forming a so-called reinforced concrete structure. This hierarchical structure of nanoparticles assembled into micro-sheets creates uniform conductive pathways between poorly conductive TiO<sub>2</sub> materials, which is beneficial to improving their electrical conductivity. Besides, the intertwined CNT networks can effectively enhance the mechanical strength of electrical current structures, and the nanoparticles can provide channels for the diffusion of electrolytes.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202301996-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/eb5f0893-ce43-456e-8d95-8252c87c7602/adfm202301996-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/eb5f0893-ce43-456e-8d95-8252c87c7602/adfm202301996-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c9647d6c-a5c8-41d9-b346-81c7328e7beb/adfm202301996-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/eb5f0893-ce43-456e-8d95-8252c87c7602/adfm202301996-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202301996-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202301996" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphology and structure characterizations of CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub>. a) The advantages of high-mass-loading CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode compared with traditional electrode fabrication process. b,c) SEM images. d) TEM image after a long-time sonication treatment. e) XRD pattern, and f) Raman spectrum. g) EPR spectrum. High-resolution XPS spectra of h) Ti 2<i>p</i>, i) O 1<i>s</i>, j) S 2<i>p</i>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> is detected in the XRD pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0001">1e</a>) and the Raman spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0001">1f</a>) of CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub>. The peak ≈150&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> is slightly blue-shifted compared with commercial TiO<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), indicating the presence of oxygen vacancies (OVs) or other defects introduced during the high-temperature calcination in the reducing atmosphere.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0015_R_d81318288e696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Stor. Mater. Chem Asian J">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Electron Paramagnetic Resonance (EPR) spectrum also confirms the presence of OVs in the composite (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0001">1g</a>). The high-resolution XPS Ti 2<i>p</i> spectrum suggests that a considerable amount of Ti<sup>4+</sup> is converted to Ti<sup>3+</sup> to compensate for the charge accumulation caused by oxygen deficiency (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0001">1h</a>). Therefore, it comes with the peak at ≈531.7&nbsp;eV in the O 1<i>s</i> spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0001">1i</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0016_R_d81318288e720" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Electrochim. Acta">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0001">1j</a>, the peaks located at 163.6 and 164.8&nbsp;eV could be assigned to S occupying the position of O in TiO<sub>2</sub>, while the peaks at 167.4 and 168.6&nbsp;eV could be attributed to S occupying the position of Ti in TiO<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0017_R_d81318288e732" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, energy dispersive spectra (EDS) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) and element mapping (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information) further evidence the presence of S in CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub>. The abundant CC inside the CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> is from SWCNT, contributing to the good electron conductivity of the electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> maintains a high specific surface area of up to 80&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information), which provides plenty of active sites for Na storage.</p>
         
         <p>To assess the potential of CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> as an energy storage material in NIBs, a series of electrochemical tests such as cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) were performed using SWCNTs as a binder and conductive agent (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0002">2</a>). The CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode contained up to 90% S-TiO<sub>2</sub> by weight, which would facilitate the high areal capacity. The CV curves of the initial cycle show a large peak located at ≈0.2&nbsp;V, which gradually disappears along with the cycles. This irreversible peak mainly comes from the decomposition of electrolyte for the formation of a stable solid electrolyte interface &nbsp;and other side reactions at the initial several cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0002">2a</a>). After the first several cycles, the CV curve displayed the characteristic sodium storage behavior of TiO<sub>2</sub> with an reduction peak at 0.75&nbsp;V (Ti<sup>4+</sup>→Ti<sup>3+</sup>) and an oxidation peak at 0.85&nbsp;V (Ti<sup>3+</sup>→Ti<sup>4+</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0018_R_d81318288e790" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The GCD voltage profiles in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0002">2b</a> agree well with the CV results. The CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode, with the mass loading of 1.96&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, delivered a discharge capacity of 231.9&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and a charge capacity of 183.4&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, resulting in a high Initial Coulombic Efficiency (ICE) of 79.0%. In addition, the voltage profiles of CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes showed slight differences from the TiO<sub>2</sub>-CMC-AB electrode (consisting of 90&nbsp;wt.% commercial TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information), 5&nbsp;wt.% Sodium carboxymethylcellulose (CMC), and 5&nbsp;wt.% Acetylene black (AB) due to the sodium storage of SWCNT (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). The rate performance of the CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> anode, with a mass loading of 1.96&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0002">2c</a>. Their specific capacity was determined to be 196, 190, 186, 180, 174, 170, 158, 152, and 142&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at corresponding rates of 0.3, 0.6, 0.9, 1.5, 2.2, 3.0, 6.0, 9.0, and 15&nbsp;C (1&nbsp;C&nbsp;=&nbsp;335&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), respectively. This result is much better than that of commercial TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles, which only delivered a capacity of 25&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at the rate of 15&nbsp;C. In addition, these performances are also superior to a series of reported TiO<sub>2</sub>-based anodes in NIBs (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0002">2d</a> shows the voltage profiles of CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode at different rates, where the voltage behavior remained relatively stable even with increasing current density. In contrast, the TiO<sub>2</sub>-CMC-AB electrode exhibited severe polarization (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information) at high rates owing to the limitations in electronic conductivity. Furthermore, the CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode retained a high capacity of ≈124&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> even after 10&nbsp;000 times at a high current density of 15&nbsp;C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0002">2e</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202301996-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ee62ba1d-eaa8-4fba-b072-cd3fecb22a4c/adfm202301996-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ee62ba1d-eaa8-4fba-b072-cd3fecb22a4c/adfm202301996-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e635d6ce-7bc9-48d7-8935-7b7304ef5acc/adfm202301996-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ee62ba1d-eaa8-4fba-b072-cd3fecb22a4c/adfm202301996-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202301996-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202301996" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performances of CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> in NIBs. a) CV curves, b) GCD voltage profiles of CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub>. c) Rate performances. d) Voltage profiles of CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> at various current densities. e) Long-term cycling performance. f) CV curves at different scan rates. Capacitive contributions of CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> at g) 0.8&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> and h) other scan rates.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">To disclose the insights into the superior rate performance mechanism of CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub>, CV curves at different scan rates were conducted to analyze its electrochemical reaction kinetics (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0002">2f</a>). As the scan rate increases, the potential of the cathodic and anodic peaks on the curves does not shift significantly, indicating a slight polarization potential of the electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0019_R_d81318288e898" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> All the curves share a similar rectangular shape, demonstrating the presence of a surface diffusion process in the electrode. To estimate the pseudocapacitive capacities, the experimental data were fitted based on Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202301996-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>):

            <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202301996-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/7afa4bb3-ee57-4f34-b6b2-9099ec97245c/adfm202301996-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202301996:adfm202301996-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202301996-math-0001.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mtable displaystyle="true">
                           <mtr>
                              <mtd columnalign="right">
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi>i</mi>
                                    <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                    <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>k</mi>
                                       <mn>1</mn>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mi>v</mi>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>k</mi>
                                       <mn>2</mn>
                                    </msub>
                                    <msup>
                                       <mi>v</mi>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mn>1</mn>
                                          <mo>/</mo>
                                          <mn>2</mn>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </msup>
                                    <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                 </mrow>
                              </mtd>
                           </mtr>
                        </mtable>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{eqnarray*}i\ = {k}_1v + {k}_2{v}^{1/2}\ \end{eqnarray*}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>Here, <i>i</i> is the cathodic or anodic current, <i>v</i> denotes the scan rate, and <i>k<sub>1</sub></i> and <i>k<sub>2</sub></i> are adjustable constants. Notably, the pseudo-capacitance ratio accounts for 88.8% at the scan rate of 0.8&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0002">2g</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0002">2h</a> presents a summary of the capacitive contributions of CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> at various scan rates. The pseudocapacitive-controlled behavior facilitates rapid sodiation/de-sodiation and enables a long cycling life.</p>
         
         <p>Based on the highly conductive 2D sheet structure and superior kinetic properties of CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub>, a series of high-mass-loading electrodes were investigated to evaluate its practical application in SIBs. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0003">3a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information) summarize the coulombic efficiency values during the initial cycle of different mass-loading electrodes. The ICE values did not significantly decrease with increased loading. The values of specific capacity displayed by these electrodes were also be evaluated. Generally, capacity degradation occurs with increasing the mass loading because of the progressively hindered electron and ion transport with the increasing thickness. The voltage profiles in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0003">3b</a> exhibit slight capacity loss and overpotential values&nbsp;(Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0003">3a</a>) with increasing mass loadings, indicating a loading-independent charge transfer resistance for CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub>. Moreover, 91.9% capacity retention of the thin electrode (1.4&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>) is still kept when the mass loading increases to 21.2&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, indicating a relatively sufficient reaction occurred in the thick electrode. Furthermore, the electrode (21.2&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>) displayed a high areal specific capacity of 3.4&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> after cycling for over 100 times at a current density of 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0003">3c</a>), exceeding the actual application indicators (3&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>) and a series of other works of TiO<sub>2</sub>-based anode (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The initial increase in capacity may be attributed to the infiltration of the electrolyte into the electrode and the activation process of the high loading electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0020_R_d81318288e1009" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J Energy Chem ACS Nano">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the areal capacity of 0.3, 0.8, 1.3, 2.2, and 3.7&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> was obtained for mass loading of 1.4, 4.3, 7.0, 12.1, and 21.2&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> (discharge capacity of the 20<sup>th</sup> cycle), respectively. The relationship between mass loading and areal capacity of the CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode demonstrates a rough linear fit, indicating its potential use in high-mass-loading electrodes. Furthermore, the fast charge–discharge performance has been explored as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0003">3d,e</a>. A capacity of ≈94&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is maintained with 7.6&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> loading at a high rate of 9&nbsp;C (≈3&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), demonstrating the fast electron and ion transport process along with the architecture of CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202301996-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3266560d-d742-4346-8258-3799c5f098d7/adfm202301996-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3266560d-d742-4346-8258-3799c5f098d7/adfm202301996-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/115735b4-84a7-47b6-85de-65fe16f864e1/adfm202301996-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3266560d-d742-4346-8258-3799c5f098d7/adfm202301996-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202301996-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202301996" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performances of high-mass-loading electrodes of CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub>. a) The ICE values of the initial cycle and the overpotential values of the 20<sup>th</sup> cycle. b) Voltage profiles of the 20<sup>th</sup> cycle. c) The plot of mass loading versus areal capacity and cycling performances. d) Rate performances and e) corresponding voltage profiles at various current densities.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The excellent electrochemical performances of electrodes with high mass loadings benefit from the enhanced charge transport (both electron and ion) of the whole electrode architecture. For the electroactive material of S-TiO<sub>2</sub>, sulfur doping and OVs can narrow its bandgap, and thus increase the electronic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0017_R_d81318288e1065" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Density of States (DOSs) theoretical calculations were conducted for both TiO<sub>2</sub> and S-TiO<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0004">4a,b</a>), which demonstrated that S-TiO<sub>2</sub> had a narrower bandgap (1.47&nbsp;eV) than that of TiO<sub>2</sub> (1.83&nbsp;eV) after sulfur doping and OV induction.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202301996-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/96211c04-8b7c-4279-adf9-cd500ebc9614/adfm202301996-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/96211c04-8b7c-4279-adf9-cd500ebc9614/adfm202301996-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/006fbcba-1c93-43c7-a79c-47fd30bb730f/adfm202301996-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/96211c04-8b7c-4279-adf9-cd500ebc9614/adfm202301996-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202301996-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202301996" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Evolution of kinetic properties of high mass loading electrodes. a,b) Calculated DOS figures. c) Conductivity values as a function of SWCNTs content in the CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode. d) Areal loading versus electrode resistance plot. e) Barrier of Na diffusion in pristine TiO<sub>2</sub> and S-TiO<sub>2</sub>. f) GITT curves and g) the corresponding diffusion constant of CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode and TiO<sub>2</sub>-CMC-AB electrodes. h,i) Comparison of Nyquist plots obtained from EIS tested by the EIS-SCs at a SOC 0% for CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub>, CNT/TiO<sub>2</sub>, and TiO<sub>2</sub>-CMC-AB electrodes and corresponding <i>R</i><sub>ion</sub> values.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">The utilization of SWCNTs in the preparation of thick electrodes offers significant advantages compared with the traditional binder and conductive agent. With the increased thickness of the electrode, the common binder, such as CMC, is easy to reach its critical cracking thickness, resulting in the evident breakage and detachment from the collector. While CNTs play an important role in fiber toughening for CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode slurry, thus enhancing the stability and maintaining the integrity of thick electrodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). The thicknesses of CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes (10&nbsp;wt.% SWCNT) with various mass loadings were measured using a thickness gauge, and a positive correlation was observed between the thickness and the mass loading (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). To further investigate the electron and ion transportation ability of CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode, several tests were conducted. The electrical conductivity values of CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes were measured using the four-probe technique, which were much higher than that of TiO<sub>2</sub>-CMC-AB electrode (5&nbsp;wt.% CMC and 5&nbsp;wt.% AB), demonstrating the superior electronic conductivity of SWCNTs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0004">4c</a>). However, the measured <i>R</i><sub>electrode</sub> of CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode was inversely proportional to the thickness, because the increased thickness of the electrode provides a larger electrochemical surface area. To enable a fair comparison of <i>R</i><sub>electrode</sub> with various thicknesses,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0021_R_d81318288e1157" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> electrode resistance per unit electroactive surface area (<i>R</i><sub>electrode, A</sub>) can be expressed as Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202301996-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>):

            <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202301996-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/5db27de3-16e9-4096-85e0-0f7acecba0fd/adfm202301996-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202301996:adfm202301996-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202301996-math-0002.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mtable displaystyle="true">
                           <mtr>
                              <mtd columnalign="right">
                                 <mrow>

                                          <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>

                                       </mrow>

                                    <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                    <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                    <mrow>

                                       <mi>R</mi>

                                          <mi>e</mi>

                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{eqnarray*}{R}_{electrode,\ A} = \ 2{\rm{\pi rL}}{R}_{electrode}\end{eqnarray*}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">In which r is the electrode radius while L is the electrode thickness. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0004">4d</a> demonstrates that the <i>R</i><sub>electrode, A</sub> did not increase much as increase the mass loading, indicating that the thickness did not significantly affect the electron transport within the electrode due to the homogeneous conductive CNTs network. In addition to the electronic conductivity, the ionic conductivity within the thick electrode is equally important. Therefore, the activation energy (Ea) was calculated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0004">4e</a>) to evaluate the transport ability of Na atom based on the diffusion pathway of Na atoms from stable adsorption site to sub-stable adsorption site on TiO<sub>2</sub> and S-TiO<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0010_R_d81318288e1236" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J Energy Chem E.Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0022_R_d81318288e1236" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Res.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After the introduction of S-doping and OVs, the activation energy of Na diffusion in S-TiO<sub>2</sub> (0.58&nbsp;eV) is much smaller than that of TiO<sub>2</sub> (0.92&nbsp;eV). Furthermore, GITT measurements were conducted with a mass loading of ≈5&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> to investigate the ion transport kinetics of electrodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0004">4f</a>). The first pulse behavior of the charging process (the arrow position) showed that the IR compensation of the TiO<sub>2</sub>-CMC-AB electrode was higher than that of the CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode, which agrees well with the measured electrode conductivity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0004">4c</a>). The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) were calculated based on Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202301996-disp-0003" title="Link to equation">3</a>),<sup>[</sup><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202301996-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/dc911c82-7f40-4288-855c-e111432425dd/adfm202301996-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202301996:adfm202301996-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202301996-math-0003.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mtable displaystyle="true">
                           <mtr>
                              <mtd columnalign="right">
                                 <mrow>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>D</mi>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>N</mi>
                                          <msup>
                                             <mi>a</mi>
                                             <mo>+</mo>
                                          </msup>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                    <mfrac>
                                       <mn>4</mn>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>π</mi>
                                          <mi>τ</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfrac>
                                    <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                    <msup>
                                       <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                          <mfrac>
                                             <mrow>
                                                <mi>m</mi>
                                                <msub>
                                                   <mi>V</mi>
                                                   <mi>m</mi>
                                                </msub>
                                             </mrow>
                                             <mrow>
                                                <mi>M</mi>
                                                <mi>A</mi>
                                             </mrow>
                                          </mfrac>
                                       </mfenced>
                                       <mn>2</mn>
                                    </msup>
                                    <msup>
                                       <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                          <mfrac>
                                             <mrow>
                                                <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                                <msub>
                                                   <mi>E</mi>
                                                   <mi>S</mi>
                                                </msub>
                                             </mrow>
                                             <mrow>
                                                <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                                <msub>
                                                   <mi>E</mi>
                                                   <mi>t</mi>
                                                </msub>
                                             </mrow>
                                          </mfrac>
                                       </mfenced>
                                       <mn>2</mn>
                                    </msup>
                                    <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                    <mi>τ</mi>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">≪</mo>
                                    <msup>
                                       <mi>L</mi>
                                       <mn>2</mn>
                                    </msup>
                                    <mo>/</mo>
                                    <mi>D</mi>
                                 </mrow>
                              </mtd>
                           </mtr>
                        </mtable>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{eqnarray*}{D}_{N{a}^ + } = \frac{4}{{\pi \tau }}\ {\left( {\frac{{m{V}_m}}{{MA}}} \right)}^2{\left( {\frac{{\Delta {E}_S}}{{\Delta {E}_t}}} \right)}^2\ \tau \ll {L}^2/D\end{eqnarray*}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>where <i>τ</i> is pulse duration, <i>M</i> is the molar mass, <i>m</i> is the mass of the active materials, <i>A</i> is the electrode active surface area, <i>V</i><sub>m</sub> is the molar volume, and <i>ΔE</i><sub>s</sub> and <i>ΔE</i><sub>t</sub> represent the potential change of quasi-equilibrium potential and during the constant current pulse. The D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> value of the CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode is higher than that of the TiO<sub>2</sub>-CMC-AB electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0004">4g</a>), which is due to the crystal defects introduced by active sites and the unique electrode architecture benefiting the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. More importantly, the highly conductive structure formed by CNTs can provide a pathway for the rapid flux of sodium ions into the interior of the electrode, while the TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles assembled sheets facilitate the transport of Na<sup>+</sup> toward the surface of active materials, enabling fast ionic conductivity.</div>
         
         <p>To further investigate the relationship between ionic conductivity and electrode thickness, the symmetric cells EIS (EIS-SC) test was conducted to identify the ionic resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ion</sub>) with two identical electrodes. To accurately simulate the electrochemical process occurring inside the cell, the transmission line model (TLM) is used.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202301996-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202301996-bib-0024_R_d81318288e1376" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Phys. Chem. C">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> There is no faradic process at 0% SOC as evidenced by the 45<sup>o</sup> slope in the middle-frequency (≈100&nbsp;Hz) region and quasi-vertical line at low frequency. Here, R<sub>ion</sub>/3 can be calculated as the projection of the 45<sup>o</sup> slope to the real axis (Z’). To make the comparison result more convincing, the CNT/TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode was prepared using the commercial TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles as active material and SWCNTs as a binder and conductive agent. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0004">4h</a> shows that the <i>R</i><sub>ion</sub> of the CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode is lower than that of TiO<sub>2</sub>-CMC-AB and CNT/TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode with the same mass loading of 4.7&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, which agrees well with the GITT results, confirming that the sulfur-doping and unique highly conductive hierarchical structure enhance the ion migration process. Particularly, with an increase in thickness and mass loading for the CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode, the ionic conductivity remains stable (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0004">4i</a>). Therefore, the mass transport including both ions and electrons can remain in a stable state, and the percolation CNTs architecture of the CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode could maintain excellent cycling and high rate capacity even at such a high mass loading.</p>
         
         <p>The ability to achieve high loading of CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes in NIBs can be attributed to the high electrode stability obtained by the use of SWCNT, which serves as both binder and conductive matrix. The flat electrode surface coated with carbon nanotubes indicates that a highly homogenous slurry was obtained with the electrode fabrication process (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0005">5a</a>). Cross-sectional SEM images taken at various loading levels (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0005">5b</a>) displayed the intimate contact between the current collector and the electrode material, which is facilitated by the inherent stickiness of SWCNT. A high-magnification SEM image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0005">5c</a>) revealed a unique pore structure where SWCNTs were not tightly packed between the active materials, but instead form aggregates that wrap around the particles. The interconnected SWCNT provides a regular conductive framework and achieves continuous long-distance charge transfer. This porous morphology greatly facilitates mass transportation ability for CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes and confirms the good electrochemical performances even under high loading electrodes. However, the isolated pore structure does not appear in the CNT/TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0005">5d</a>) and TiO<sub>2</sub>-CMC-AB (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0005">5e</a>) electrode. Furthermore, the traditional thick electrode with CMC, AB, and nano-sized TiO<sub>2</sub> would suffer not only from the long ion transportation pathways but also from the insufficient charge transfer process (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). The relationship between electrode structure and Na<sup>+</sup>/electron transport is summarized in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0005">5f</a>. To further detect the structure stability of electrodes, SEM images after cycling were also collected (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0005">5g–i</a>). The unique porous structure constructed in the CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode remains intact (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202301996-fig-0005">5g</a>) even after 100&nbsp;cycles, illustrating the great stability of the electrode.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202301996-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0e8e5849-d794-4d20-b5fa-7a2258628171/adfm202301996-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0e8e5849-d794-4d20-b5fa-7a2258628171/adfm202301996-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b03ddfa4-48ea-44b0-84c0-02b0d7babdf5/adfm202301996-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0e8e5849-d794-4d20-b5fa-7a2258628171/adfm202301996-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202301996-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202301996" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM images of the a) top-view and b) side-view for CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes. Room-in side-view SEM images of c,g) CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode, d,h) CNT/TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode, and e,i) TiO<sub>2</sub>-CMC-AB electrode c,d,e) before cycle and g,h,i) after 100 cycles. f) Schematic diagram of the relationship between electrode structure and Na<sup>+</sup>/electron transport.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202301996-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202301996-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion&nbsp;</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, our studies illustrate that the hierarchical structure composed of SWCNT-derivatized micro-sheets and TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles is essential for achieving effective charge transport. The introduction of OVs and S-doping during the preparation process significantly compensates for the poor electrical conductivity of TiO<sub>2</sub>-based materials. The use of SWCNT as a binder and conductive agent allows for a high active materials content of 90%. Moreover, the micro-size of CNT/S-TiO<sub>2</sub> facilitates the formation of a highly conductive structure. The resulting electrode enables stable cycling with a high-mass-loading of 21.2&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, thanks to the unique pore structure introduced by the isolation of micro-size particles, which greatly improves the ability of ion transport within the thick electrode. This work provides a valuable reference for the preparation of thick electrodes with high conductivity and high ion transport properties using nanomaterials.</p>
         </section>
